Buyer's Claim of Deficiency Over Time: Silk and Acknowledgment
Ruling on buyer's later claim of deficiency and effect of acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for a buyer's later claim of deficiency, distinguishing natural loss from other losses, and the effect of acknowledgment.

Core Idea
Buyer's claim of deficiency after delivery is accepted only if not contradicted by acknowledgment or natural causes.

The third ruling from al-Khaniyyah deals with a buyer who claims silk is deficient after a period. The ruling turns on two factors: the cause of the loss (natural or typical) and whether the buyer acknowledged receiving a specific weight at delivery. The overarching principle 'al-qawl li-l-qabid' (the word of the possessor) is applied, but qualified by acknowledgment. This lesson unpacks these nuances and their practical impact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and claim
A buyer purchases silk, seller weighs it in front of buyer, buyer takes it. Later, buyer claims deficiency. Determine the timeline and circumstances.
The claim is made after a period, not immediately.
2
Examine the cause of loss
If the loss is natural (e.g., evaporation, 'from the air') or typical between two weighings (e.g., normal shrinkage), seller is not liable.
The burden is on buyer to show the loss is not natural.
3
If loss is not natural, check acknowledgment
If the buyer did not acknowledge receiving a specific weight, his word is accepted, and he can withhold or recover the deficiency amount.
This applies 'al-qawl li-l-qabid'.
4
If buyer acknowledged, he forfeits right
If the buyer explicitly said 'I received such-and-such mann,' he cannot later claim less. He is bound by his acknowledgment.
Acknowledgment (iqrar) is binding; it overrides the default rule.
5
Summarize the conditions
The buyer's claim is accepted only if: (a) loss is not natural/typical, (b) no acknowledgment. Otherwise, buyer has no recourse.
This balances interests: seller not liable for normal changes, but seller must compensate if deficiency is due to original underweight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الخانية : رجل باع لؤلؤة على أنها تزن مثقالاً فوجدها أكثر سلمت للمشتري لأن الوزن فيما يضره التبعيض وصف بمنزلة الذرعان في الثوب اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : اشترى طشتاً على أنه عشرة أمناء فبان بعد القبض أنه خمسة أمناء خير المشتري لأنه بمنزلة العيب، فإن حدث به عيب عنده وأبى البائع قبوله قوم طشت من عشرة أمناء مثلاً بعشرين، وقوم من خمسة أمناء بعشرة أمناء فالعيب ينقص خمسة ا هـ . والقول للقابض في الزيادة والنقصان وعليها يتفرع ما في الخانية : ولو باع من آخر إبريسماً فوزنه البائع على المشتري فذهب به المشتري ثم جاء بعد مدة وقال وجدته ناقصاً ؛ إن كان يعلم أنه انتقص من الهواء لا شيء على البائع، وكذا لو كان النقصان مما يجري بين الوزنين وإن لم يكن النقصان من الهواء ولا يجري بين الوزنين فإن لم يكن المشتري أقر أنه قبض كذا أمناء فله أن يمنع حصة النقصان من الثمن إن كان لم ينقده الثمن، فإن كان نقده الثمن رجع عليه بذلك القدر، وإن كان المشتري أقر أنه قبض كذا وجدته أقل من ذلك فليس له أن يمنع من البائع شيئاً من الثمن ولا يسترده اهـ
He said in al-Khaniyyah: A man sold a pearl on the basis that it weighs one mithqal, and he found it more; it is conceded to the buyer because weight in what harms division is a description like the cubit in cloth, end. And in al-Khulasah: He bought a basin on the basis that it is ten mann, and it became clear after possession that it is five mann; the buyer has an option because it is like a defect. Then if a defect occurred in his possession and the seller refused to accept it, the basin is valued as ten mann, for example, at twenty, and valued as five mann at ten; so the defect is a reduction of five, end. And the word is of the possessor regarding increase and decrease. Upon it branches what is in al-Khaniyyah: And if one sold to another silk, and the seller weighed it for the buyer, and the buyer took it, then came after a period and said, 'I found it deficient': if he knows that it decreased from the air, nothing is on the seller. Likewise if the decrease is of the sort that occurs between two weighings. And if the decrease is not from the air and does not occur between two weighings, then if the buyer has not acknowledged that he received such-and-such mann, he may withhold the share of the decrease from the price if he has not paid the price; if he has paid the price, he recovers that amount from him. And if the buyer acknowledged that he received such-and-such and found it less than that, he may not withhold anything from the seller from the price nor recover it, end.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings from Hanafi texts. The first, from al-Khaniyyah, concerns a pearl sold with a specified weight (one mithqal). If the buyer finds it heavier, the excess belongs to the buyer because for items like pearls, weight is a description akin to the cubit measurement in cloth; an excess does not harm the contract—the buyer gets the extra without additional payment. The second, from al-Khulasah, concerns a basin sold as ten mann but found to be five after possession. The buyer has an option because deficiency is like a defect. If a subsequent defect occurs in the buyer's possession and the seller refuses to take it back, the compensation is calculated: value of ten-mann basin minus value of five-mann basin, and the buyer recovers that difference. The third ruling, from al-Khaniyyah, deals with a buyer's later claim of deficiency in silk after possession. The key factors: if the loss is natural (from air) or typical between weighings, the seller is not liable. Otherwise, if the buyer did not acknowledge receipt of a specific weight, his word is accepted, and he can withhold or recover the deficiency. But if he acknowledged receipt, he cannot later claim deficiency.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Legal acknowledgment by the buyer that he received a specific weight; binding and prevents later claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the silk ruling, if the buyer acknowledged receiving 10 mann but later finds it only 9 mann, what can he do?
A) He can withhold payment for the missing mann.
B) He can recover the value of the missing mann if he paid.
C) He can do nothing because acknowledgment binds him.
D) He can return the silk for a full refund.

Key Takeaway
Buyer's later claim of deficiency is accepted only if loss is not natural and buyer did not acknowledge receiving the specified weight.

The Effect of Customary Pricing on Deficiency Claims
Ruling on deficiency recovery based on local custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a local buyer recovers deficiency in both bread and meat when town pricing is customary, while a non-local only recovers in bread, and identify the basis in custom ('urf).

Core Idea
Customary pricing binds locals fully, but non-locals only for staples like bread.

In markets where town authorities or widespread custom fix prices for certain commodities, the buyer's ability to claim refund for a deficiency depends on whether they are from that town. This passage from al-Bazzaziyyah illustrates the distinction between bread and meat, and how custom ('urf) influences the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commodity and the custom
The town has fixed prices for bread and meat. Determine which commodities have established customary pricing.
Bread pricing is more uniform than meat.
2
Classify the buyer
Is the buyer a resident of the town or an outsider? This determines the scope of their right to claim deficiency.
Locals are bound by the common usage; outsiders are not fully bound.
3
Apply the ruling
If local: can claim deficiency in both bread and meat. If non-local: only in bread because meat pricing varies.
The distinction is based on the pervasiveness of the custom.
4
Derive the principle
The ruling shows that customary prevailing rates are binding on those who are aware of them, but not on those unaware.
This is a subset of the Hanafi principle that custom is considered law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: The people of a town agreed on the price of bread and meat and it became widespread such that there is no variation. Then a man gave a price and bought it and gave less than the customary: if he is from the townspeople, he recovers the deficiency in both from the price; if he is not from its people, he recovers in bread because pricing in it is customary so it binds everyone, not in meat, so no. And by analogy with the saying of the Imam, the contract becomes invalid in the remainder, and the Qadi of the Two Sanctuaries narrated from the Imam regarding what is found, and he gave fatwa accordingly. And al-Halwani and al-Sarakhsi gave fatwa that the contract is of the type of mann, then he said, it is valid, general, al-Shahid. And in al-Muhit: He bought half of what is in the specific vineyard of grapes on the vine on the basis that it is five hundred mann, permitted? he found that amount or less or more. And al-Lamishi mentioned that it is only permissible if he finds five hundred; and if he said, 'I sold a thousand mann from this vineyard' if the grapes are of one type, it is permissible. And in al-Multaqat: The permissibility of buying grapes from the vineyard if it is such-and-such baskets and he mentioned them, and the appraisers look. end. Named for estimation of value. Then if he stipulates that it is such-and-such baskets, it is permissible with the conditions of salam, otherwise no. And the buyer is liable for what he destroys and nothing on him of the price of the remainder if the contract is valid, and it is not stipulated to mention them and their number. So if he finds it more or less, nothing for either on the other because he bought the whole without estimation. And in al-Muhit: If he buys a kurr on the basis that it is ten qafiz, and he measures it and finds it more than ten, the excess is for the seller because the amount sold is ten qafiz; then if he measures it a second time and finds it deficient, he does not complete it because the amount of the sale has become clear by the first measurement and it has become delivered, so the second measurement is not considered. And if he measures it and finds it less than ten, he deducts from its price, and if he wishes he takes the remainder for its share of the price, and if he wishes he leaves it. Then if he measures it a second time and finds it ten, it increases upon the price and his option is not invalidated, and consideration is to the first measurement. end. And from it the ruling of weighed items is known. And in Talkhis al-Jami': Chapter of buying a container with its contents and food and fungible items: He bought a skin of oil with its contents on the basis that both are one hundred pounds, and the skin is heavier than the measure? he has an option because of estimation. And if it is twenty, he deducts the price specific to the oil if the oil is seventy after dividing the price over the value of oil or the value of eighty pounds of oil, and the option and return of twenty if it is one hundred, proportioning the deficiency and surplus to the oil because quantity is the foundation in it without the skin, as if he said: the skin is as found and the oil completes the hundred. And if instead of the skin there is ghee, he deducts three-fifths of what pertains to it and returns two-sevenths of the oil after dividing the price over the value of fifty of each individual because quantity is the foundation in both so they share it, as in the sale by a thousand mithqal of gold and silver. And if the skin is one hundred and the oil fifty, it is invalid due to ignorance of the price or condition of non-existent, since there is no reduction in the skin and no contract in other than the hundred. And if he bought ten sheep and ten qafiz on the basis that each sheep and qafiz is for a dirham, then the qafiz are nine, he returns all because the transaction is not complete, or he deducts the ten share of the food after dividing each dirham over a sheep and a qafiz and proceeds, because ignorance is removed by assuming equality. And if the sheep are nine, it is invalid in the qafiz according to them two, and in all according to him because of the condition of riba if there is no countervalue for what is lost in money, and its completion is in it. And 'al-ziqq' with kasr is the container; thus in al-Misbah. He made the option absolute at deficiency from what he named. And Qadikhan restricted it in his Fatawa and said: And look at what we wrote there: His saying: And Qadikhan restricted it in his Fatawa etc. He said in al-Nahr: You are aware that the cause for the option is only the splitting of the transaction, and this extent is established in what he finds after possession deficient, except if it is said that by possession he became satisfied with that, so ponder it. end. I say: And look at the previous statement of the author and the transmitted that the possessor's word is in increase and decrease to the end of what he quoted from al-Khaniyyah there, for it indicates that mere possession without acknowledgment does not prevent the option. But it may be distinguished that what passed is when the seller denies the deficiency unlike here. And what is appropriate to say: if the buyer knew of the deficiency before possession, he has no right to return because of his satisfaction with splitting the transaction; if he did not know until after it, he has the right to return. Ponder his saying: (And if he possessed the whole, he does not have the option). He said in al-Nahr: meaning he only recovers the deficiency.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers multiple rulings from various Hanafi sources on options for deficiency in agricultural produce, containers with contents, and general rules. First, it discusses the effect of custom on pricing: in a town with fixed prices, a local buyer gets refund for deficiency in both bread and meat, but a non-local only for bread because meat pricing is not customary. Second, for buying grapes from a vineyard by estimated quantity: if actual is less, the seller demands proportional price; some scholars invalidate the contract for the remainder, while others uphold it. Third, buying half of a vineyard's yield on estimate: validity depends on whether the estimated amount is found; if the type is uniform, it may be valid. Fourth, buying grapes by baskets: conditions of salam apply. Fifth, buying a kurr of grain: if found more, excess belongs to seller; if less, buyer may deduct or take remainder proportionately; second measurement is not considered after possession. Sixth, buying a container with oil: the option exists due to estimation; if the container is heavier than expected, buyer can adjust; if the container is the major component, allocation differs. Seventh, buying sheep and grain together: if one component is deficient, either the whole sale is returned or price is reallocated assuming equality. The passage concludes with a discussion from al-Nahr and the author's own analysis: the option for deficiency after possession depends on whether the buyer knew before or after possession; mere possession without knowledge does not invalidate the option. The key principle is that the cause of the option is the splitting of the transaction (tafriq al-safqah).

Key Terms
المسعر (al-mus'ir): Fixed price set by custom or authority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a non-local buys bread in a town with fixed bread prices, can he claim a deficiency?
A) Yes
B) No
C) Only if he is from the same country
D) Only if meat is also fixed

Key Takeaway
Customary pricing that is pervasive binds all participants, but the scope of binding differs for locals and non-locals based on the commodity.

10 min
Option for Deficiency in Estimated Purchases: Grapes and Grain
Rulings on option in estimated grape and grain purchases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the option for deficiency when purchasing grapes or grain by estimated quantity, and distinguish between Abu Hanifa's view and that of al-Halwani/al-Sarakhsi.

Core Idea
When estimated quantity is deficient, the buyer may have an option; Abu Hanifa invalidates the remainder, later scholars uphold the contract.

This passage continues with rulings from al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Muhit on buying grapes from a vineyard and grain by measure. When the actual quantity differs from the estimated, the buyer's options vary. An intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is reported between Abu Hanifa and later scholars regarding the validity of the remainder of the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purchase type
The purchase is based on an estimated quantity (e.g., a thousand mann of grapes, a kurr of grain).
Estimation is common for produce not yet harvested.
2
Determine the actual quantity relative to estimate
If actual is less than estimated, a deficiency exists. If more, the excess is considered.
Different rules apply for deficiency vs excess.
3
Analyze the Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa (by qiyas) invalidates the contract for the remainder; al-Halwani and al-Sarakhsi uphold the contract and allow the buyer to take the remainder at a proportional price.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
4
Apply the rule for grain (kurr)
From al-Muhit: if a kurr is sold as ten qafiz and found more, excess goes to seller; if less, buyer may take remainder or leave, and second measurement is not considered after possession.
This parallels the grape case but with additional details.
5
Identify the cause (tafriq al-safqah)
The option arises because the transaction is split: part of the goods is delivered, part is not.
Ta'liq of option is due to split transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الشهيد. وفي المحيط : اشترى نصف ما في الكرم المعين من العنب الذي على الكرم على أنه خمسمائة من بجوز، وجد ذلك القدر أو أقل أو أكثر. وذكر اللامشي أنما يجوز إذا وجد خمسمائة، ولو قال بعت ألف منّ من هذا الكرم إن كان العنب من نوع واحد يجوز. وفي الملتقط : جواز شراء العنب من الكرم إذا. أنه كذا كذا كوارة وذكرها وينظر المقومون اهـ. سمي لتقدير القيمة، فإن شرط أنها كذا كذا كوارة يجوز فيها بشرائط السلم وإلا فلا، وعلى المشتري ضمان ما أتلفه ولا شيء عليه من ثمن الباقي إذا كان العقد جائزاً، ولا يشترط فيه ذكرها وعددها، فإذا وجده زائداً أو ناقصاً لا شيء لأحدهما على الآخر لأنه اشترى الجملة بلا تقدير وفي المحيط : لو اشترى كراً على أنه عشرة أقفزة فكاله فوجده أكثر من عشرة فالزيادة للبائع لأن قدر المبيع عشرة أقفزة فإذا كاله ثانيا فوجده أنقص لا يكملها لأنه ظهر قدر المبيع بالكيل الأول وصار مسلماً فلا يعتبر الكيل الثاني، وإن كاله فوجده أنقص من عشرة يطرح من ثمنه، وإن شاء أخذ الباقي بحصته من الثمن، وإن شاء ترك، فإن كاله ثانياً فوجده عشرة يزيد على الثمن ولا يبطل خياره والعبرة للكيل الأول .اهـ ويعلم منه حكم الموزونات . وفي تلخيص الجامع باب شراء الظرف بما فيه والطعام والقيمي : اشترى زق زيت بما فيه على أنهما مائة رطل فإذا الزق أثقل من العتاد خير للتقدير، ولو كان عشرين حط ثمن ما خص الزيت إن كان الزيت سبعين بعد قسمة الثمن على قيمة الزيت أو قيمة ثمانين رطل زيت والتخيير ورد عشرين إن كان مائة صرفاً للنقص والفضل إلى الزيت إذ القدر أصل فيه دون الزق كأنه قال والزق ما وجد والزيت تكملة المائة. ولو كان مكان الزق سمن حط ثلاثة أخماس ما خصه ورد سبعي الزيت بعد قسمة الثمن على قيمة خمسين من كل فرد لأن القدر أصل فيهما فاقتسماه كما في البيع بألف مثقال ذهب وفضة، ولو كان الزق مائة والزيت خمسين فسد لجهالة الثمن أو شرط المعدوم إذ لا تنقيص في الزق ولا عقد في غير المائة. ولو اشترى الأغنام العشر والقفزان العشرة على أن كل شاة وقفيز بدرهم فإذا القفزان تسعة رد الكل إذ لم تتم الصفقة أو حط عشرة قسط الطعام بعد قسمة كل درهم على شاة وقفيز وأمضى لزوال الجهل بفرض التساوي. ولو كانت الأغنام تسعة فسد في قفيز عندهما، وفي الكل عنده لشرط الربا إذا لم يقابل قسط ما فات مالاً وتمامه فيه. والزق بالكسر الظرف؛ كذا في المصباح. أطلق في تخييره عند النقصان عما سماه. وقيده قاضيخان في فتاواه فقال : وإن وانظر ما كتبناه هناك قوله : وقيده قاضيخان في فتاواه الخ قال في النهر : أنت خبير بأن الموجب للتخيير إنما هو تفريق الصفقة وهذا القدر ثابت فيما لو وجده بعد القبض ناقصاً إلا أن يقال إنه بالقبض صار راضياً بذلك فتدبره.اهـ. قلت وانظر قول المؤلف السابق والمنقول للقابض في الزيادة والنقصان إلى آخر ما نقله عن الخانية هناك فإنه يفيد أن مجرد القبض بدون الإقرار لا يفيد منع التخيير لكن قد يفرق بأن ما مر فيما إذا أنكر البائع النقصان بخلاف ما هنا. والذي ينبغي أن يقال : إن علم المشتري بالنقصان قبل القبض لم يكن له الرد لرضاه بتفريق الصفقة، وإن لم يعلم إلا بعده كان له الرد تأمل قوله : (وإن كان قبض الكل لا يخير قال في النهر : يعني وإنما يرجع بالنقصان
And he made it absolute so it includes when the named thing is stipulated by word or by custom. For what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: The people of a town agreed on the price of bread and meat and it became widespread such that there is no variation. Then a man gave a price and bought it and gave less than the customary: if he is from the townspeople, he recovers the deficiency in both from the price; if he is not from its people, he recovers in bread because pricing in it is customary so it binds everyone, not in meat, so no. And in al-Bazzaziyyah also: He bought grapes of a vineyard on the basis that it is a thousand mann, and it appeared that it is nine hundred. The seller demands the share of one hundred of the price. And by analogy with the saying of the Imam, the contract becomes invalid in the remainder, and the Qadi of the Two Sanctuaries narrated from the Imam regarding what is found, and he gave fatwa accordingly. And al-Halwani and al-Sarakhsi gave fatwa that the contract is of the type of mann, then he said, it is valid, general, al-Shahid. And in al-Muhit: He bought half of what is in the specific vineyard of grapes on the vine on the basis that it is five hundred mann, permitted? he found that amount or less or more. And al-Lamishi mentioned that it is only permissible if he finds five hundred; and if he said, 'I sold a thousand mann from this vineyard' if the grapes are of one type, it is permissible. And in al-Multaqat: The permissibility of buying grapes from the vineyard if it is such-and-such baskets and he mentioned them, and the appraisers look. end. Named for estimation of value. Then if he stipulates that it is such-and-such baskets, it is permissible with the conditions of salam, otherwise no. And the buyer is liable for what he destroys and nothing on him of the price of the remainder if the contract is valid, and it is not stipulated to mention them and their number. So if he finds it more or less, nothing for either on the other because he bought the whole without estimation. And in al-Muhit: If he buys a kurr on the basis that it is ten qafiz, and he measures it and finds it more than ten, the excess is for the seller because the amount sold is ten qafiz; then if he measures it a second time and finds it deficient, he does not complete it because the amount of the sale has become clear by the first measurement and it has become delivered, so the second measurement is not considered. And if he measures it and finds it less than ten, he deducts from its price, and if he wishes he takes the remainder for its share of the price, and if he wishes he leaves it. Then if he measures it a second time and finds it ten, it increases upon the price and his option is not invalidated, and consideration is to the first measurement. end. And from it the ruling of weighed items is known. And in Talkhis al-Jami': Chapter of buying a container with its contents and food and fungible items: He bought a skin of oil with its contents on the basis that both are one hundred pounds, and the skin is heavier than the measure? he has an option because of estimation. And if it is twenty, he deducts the price specific to the oil if the oil is seventy after dividing the price over the value of oil or the value of eighty pounds of oil, and the option and return of twenty if it is one hundred, proportioning the deficiency and surplus to the oil because quantity is the foundation in it without the skin, as if he said: the skin is as found and the oil completes the hundred. And if instead of the skin there is ghee, he deducts three-fifths of what pertains to it and returns two-sevenths of the oil after dividing the price over the value of fifty of each individual because quantity is the foundation in both so they share it, as in the sale by a thousand mithqal of gold and silver. And if the skin is one hundred and the oil fifty, it is invalid due to ignorance of the price or condition of non-existent, since there is no reduction in the skin and no contract in other than the hundred. And if he bought ten sheep and ten qafiz on the basis that each sheep and qafiz is for a dirham, then the qafiz are nine, he returns all because the transaction is not complete, or he deducts the ten share of the food after dividing each dirham over a sheep and a qafiz and proceeds, because ignorance is removed by assuming equality. And if the sheep are nine, it is invalid in the qafiz according to them two, and in all according to him because of the condition of riba if there is no countervalue for what is lost in money, and its completion is in it. And 'al-ziqq' with kasr is the container; thus in al-Misbah. He made the option absolute at deficiency from what he named. And Qadikhan restricted it in his Fatawa and said: And look at what we wrote there: His saying: And Qadikhan restricted it in his Fatawa etc. He said in al-Nahr: You are aware that the cause for the option is only the splitting of the transaction, and this extent is established in what he finds after possession deficient, except if it is said that by possession he became satisfied with that, so ponder it. end. I say: And look at the previous statement of the author and the transmitted that the possessor's word is in increase and decrease to the end of what he quoted from al-Khaniyyah there, for it indicates that mere possession without acknowledgment does not prevent the option. But it may be distinguished that what passed is when the seller denies the deficiency unlike here. And what is appropriate to say: if the buyer knew of the deficiency before possession, he has no right to return because of his satisfaction with splitting the transaction; if he did not know until after it, he has the right to return. Ponder his saying: (And if he possessed the whole, he does not have the option). He said in al-Nahr: meaning he only recovers the deficiency.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers multiple rulings from various Hanafi sources on options for deficiency in agricultural produce, containers with contents, and general rules. First, it discusses the effect of custom on pricing: in a town with fixed prices, a local buyer gets refund for deficiency in both bread and meat, but a non-local only for bread because meat pricing is not customary. Second, for buying grapes from a vineyard by estimated quantity: if actual is less, the seller demands proportional price; some scholars invalidate the contract for the remainder, while others uphold it. Third, buying half of a vineyard's yield on estimate: validity depends on whether the estimated amount is found; if the type is uniform, it may be valid. Fourth, buying grapes by baskets: conditions of salam apply. Fifth, buying a kurr of grain: if found more, excess belongs to seller; if less, buyer may deduct or take remainder proportionately; second measurement is not considered after possession. Sixth, buying a container with oil: the option exists due to estimation; if the container is heavier than expected, buyer can adjust; if the container is the major component, allocation differs. Seventh, buying sheep and grain together: if one component is deficient, either the whole sale is returned or price is reallocated assuming equality. The passage concludes with a discussion from al-Nahr and the author's own analysis: the option for deficiency after possession depends on whether the buyer knew before or after possession; mere possession without knowledge does not invalidate the option. The key principle is that the cause of the option is the splitting of the transaction (tafriq al-safqah).

Key Terms
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the transaction when part of the goods is deficient; cause of option.
المن (mann): A unit of weight used in estimation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Muhit, if a kurr of grain sold as 10 qafiz is first measured and found to be 9 qafiz, what options does the buyer have?
A) He must take the 9 qafiz and pay full price.
B) He may deduct from the price or take the remainder and leave it.
C) He must return the whole consignment.
D) He can demand the seller add 1 qafiz.

Key Takeaway
In estimated purchases, a deficiency does not automatically void the sale; the preferred Hanafi view upholds the contract with proportional adjustment.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
Splitting the Transaction (Tafriq al-Safqah) in Mixed Sales
Rules for option in mixed sales due to splitting the transaction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of tafriq al-safqah to mixed sales (e.g., container with contents, sheep with grain) and determine when the buyer has an option due to deficiency.

Core Idea
The buyer's option in mixed sales arises from the splitting of the transaction (tafriq al-safqah) when one component is deficient.

This passage from Talkhis al-Jami' and the author's analysis deals with purchases where the price covers multiple items, such as a container with its contents or sheep with qafiz. When one component is deficient, the transaction is partly fulfilled and partly not, giving the buyer an option. The principle of tafriq al-safqah is identified as the cause. The author also discusses the effect of possession and knowledge on the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mixed sale structure
The sale bundles two items (e.g., oil skin + oil, sheep + qafiz) for a single price or per-unit price.
Each item has its own quantity or weight.
2
Determine deficiency in one component
The actual quantity of one item differs from the stipulated amount (e.g., skin heavier than expected, sheep fewer than stated).
Deficiency can be in either component.
3
Apply tafriq al-safqah
The transaction is split: part is delivered as agreed, part is not. The buyer gets an option to adjust or cancel.
The option is due to the transaction being split, not due to mere deficiency.
4
Calculate proportional adjustment
The price is reallocated based on the value of each component, and the buyer may either take the remainder with proportional price deduction or return the whole.
Different rules apply if quantity is the foundation (as in oil) vs both quantity and weight (as in ghee+oil).
5
Consider knowledge and possession
If the buyer knows of the deficiency before possession and accepts, the option lapses. If unaware until after possession, the option remains.
Mere possession without knowledge does not imply acceptance.

I say: And look at the previous statement of the author and the transmitted that the possessor's word is in increase and decrease to the end of what he quoted from al-Khaniyyah there, for it indicates that mere possession without acknowledgment does not prevent the option. But it may be distinguished that what passed is when the seller denies the deficiency unlike here. And what is appropriate to say: if the buyer knew of the deficiency before possession, he has no right to return because of his satisfaction with splitting the transaction; if he did not know until after it, he has the right to return. Ponder his saying: (And if he possessed the whole, he does not have the option). He said in al-Nahr: meaning he only recovers the deficiency.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers multiple rulings from various Hanafi sources on options for deficiency in agricultural produce, containers with contents, and general rules. First, it discusses the effect of custom on pricing: in a town with fixed prices, a local buyer gets refund for deficiency in both bread and meat, but a non-local only for bread because meat pricing is not customary. Second, for buying grapes from a vineyard by estimated quantity: if actual is less, the seller demands proportional price; some scholars invalidate the contract for the remainder, while others uphold it. Third, buying half of a vineyard's yield on estimate: validity depends on whether the estimated amount is found; if the type is uniform, it may be valid. Fourth, buying grapes by baskets: conditions of salam apply. Fifth, buying a kurr of grain: if found more, excess belongs to seller; if less, buyer may deduct or take remainder proportionately; second measurement is not considered after possession. Sixth, buying a container with oil: the option exists due to estimation; if the container is heavier than expected, buyer can adjust; if the container is the major component, allocation differs. Seventh, buying sheep and grain together: if one component is deficient, either the whole sale is returned or price is reallocated assuming equality. The passage concludes with a discussion from al-Nahr and the author's own analysis: the option for deficiency after possession depends on whether the buyer knew before or after possession; mere possession without knowledge does not invalidate the option. The key principle is that the cause of the option is the splitting of the transaction (tafriq al-safqah).

Key Terms
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the transaction when part of the goods is deficient; cause of option.
الظرف (al-zarf): Container (e.g., skin, jar); considered separately from contents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's analysis, when does the buyer lose the option to return the goods due to deficiency in a mixed sale?
A) Upon possession of the goods.
B) When the buyer acknowledges the deficiency after possession.
C) When the buyer knew of the deficiency before possession.
D) When the seller admits the deficiency.

Key Takeaway
The option for deficiency in mixed sales is grounded in tafriq al-safqah, and its survival depends on the buyer's knowledge at the time of possession.

Buying a Specified Measure: When Quantity Differs
Ruling from Al-Muhit on deviation in measuring

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a measured item in a sale is found to be more or less than specified, and apply the buyer's options (khiyar) accordingly.

Core Idea
The specified quantity defines the object of sale; deviation gives the buyer an option, not voidness.

In sales of measured items like grain, parties often specify a quantity. But what happens if the actual measure differs? This lesson from Al-Muhit addresses three scenarios: when the buyer finds more, less, or recovers the exact amount after a discrepancy. The key principle is that the first measurement determines the object of sale, and the buyer has a choice when the quantity is deficient. This ruling also applies to weighed items by analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
The sale is of a specific measured item (e.g., one kurr) on the condition that it equals exactly ten qafiz. The price is set for that quantity.
The specification of quantity is a condition in the contract.
2
Case 1: Actual exceeds specified
If the buyer measures and finds more than ten, the excess belongs to the seller. The buyer does not automatically own the extra; it remains the seller's property. The buyer may keep the excess only with the seller's permission or pay for it.
The object of sale is only the specified ten qafiz.
3
Case 2: Actual is less than specified
If the buyer measures and finds less than ten, he has two options: (a) deduct from the price proportionally and keep the deficient amount, or (b) reject the sale entirely.
The buyer's option (khiyar) arises here.
4
Case 3: Second measurement shows exact
If after a first (deficient) measurement a second measurement shows exactly ten, the buyer's option is not voided; the price is adjusted, and the first measurement is considered binding for determining the object of sale.
The first measurement is authoritative; later measurements do not retroactively change the contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : لو اشترى كراً على أنه عشرة أقفزة فكاله فوجده أكثر من عشرة فالزيادة للبائع لأن قدر المبيع عشرة أقفزة فإذا كاله ثانيا فوجده أنقص لا يكملها لأنه ظهر قدر المبيع بالكيل الأول وصار مسلماً فلا يعتبر الكيل الثاني، وإن كاله فوجده أنقص من عشرة يطرح من ثمنه، وإن شاء أخذ الباقي بحصته من الثمن، وإن شاء ترك، فإن كاله ثانياً فوجده عشرة يزيد على الثمن ولا يبطل خياره والعبرة للكيل الأول .اهـ ويعلم منه حكم الموزونات .
In Al-Muhit: If someone bought a ker (measure) on the condition that it is ten qafiz, then measured it and found it more than ten, the increase is for the seller because the amount of the sold item is ten qafiz. If he measured it a second time and found it deficient, he does not make it complete because the amount of the sold item became apparent by the first measurement and it became delivered, so the second measurement is not considered. If he measured it and found it less than ten, he deducts from its price; if he wishes he takes the remainder at its share of the price, and if he wishes he leaves it. If he measured it a second time and found it ten, the price increases and his option is not voided. The consideration is the first measurement. End. From it the ruling of weighed items is known.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Al-Muhit about buying a measured item (like grain) with a specified quantity. The scenario: a buyer purchases a ker (a large measure) on the condition that it contains exactly ten qafiz (a smaller measure). Three cases are addressed: (1) If the actual measure exceeds ten, the excess belongs to the seller because the object of sale was only ten; the sale is valid for ten, and the buyer may either return the excess or pay for it? Actually the text says the increase is for the seller, meaning the buyer does not have to pay for it, but he must allow the seller to take it back? Typically in Hanafi fiqh, if a measured item is found to exceed the specified amount, the excess belongs to the seller and the buyer can keep the excess only with the seller's consent or pay for it. The text here simply says 'the increase is for the seller'. (2) If the actual measure is less than ten, the buyer has a choice: either deduct from the price proportionally (ḥiṣṣa) or reject the sale entirely. (3) If after the first measurement it seemed less but a second measurement shows exactly ten, the buyer's option remains, and the price is adjusted; the first measurement is relied upon for determining the object of sale. This ruling applies by analogy to all weighed items (موزونات). The key principle: the specified quantity is the object of sale; any deviation gives the buyer an option but the sale is not void ab initio.

Key Terms
كر (kurr): A large measure of capacity, often used for grain; its exact volume varies by region.
قفزة (qafīz): A smaller measure of capacity, a fraction of a kurr.
كيل (kayl): Measurement by volume (for dry goods).
موزونات (mawzūnāt): Weighed items (as opposed to measured by volume).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a buyer purchases a kurr of wheat on the condition it contains 10 qafiz, but after measuring finds it to be 12 qafiz?
A) The sale is void because the quantity is different.
B) The buyer must pay for the extra 2 qafiz at the agreed rate.
C) The extra 2 qafiz belong to the seller; the buyer may keep them only with the seller's permission or return them.
D) The buyer has an option to either accept the extra 2 qafiz for free or return them.

Key Takeaway
When the quantity of a measured sale differs from specification, the first measurement governs; deficiency gives the buyer an option, excess belongs to the seller.

Khiyar for Deficiency: Possession, Sight, and Negligible Differences
Ruling on buyer's option for deficiency in measured/weighed items, refined by Qadikhan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-naqs when the buyer has taken full possession or seen the item, and apply the principle of tolerating small customary differences in quantity.

Core Idea
Possession and sight remove the buyer's option for deficiency; only small negligible differences are tolerated.

When you buy a measured or weighed item and find it less than specified, your right to return it (khiyar al-naqs) is not absolute. The Hanafi school, through Qadikhan and others, refined the rule: if you have taken full possession or if you saw the item at the time of purchase, you lose the option because deception (ghurur) is absent. Additionally, very small deficiencies (like half a dirham in a hundred) are overlooked and do not give you a right to return. This lesson unpacks these important qualifications.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for khiyar al-naqs
Identify the default ruling: if a measured/weighed item is deficient, the sale is valid for the found amount, and the buyer has an option to return it if he has not taken full possession.
This is the absolute ruling that Qadikhan later restricts.
2
Apply Qadikhan's restriction on possession
Distinguish between partial/no possession (option retained) and full possession (option lost).
Full possession implies the buyer has accepted the item as is.
3
Apply the effect of sight (ru'ya)
If the buyer saw the item at the time of purchase, the deficiency is known by sight, so no deception (ghurur) exists and the option is lost.
Examples: sawiq, soap, shirt – all are cases where the defect was visible.
4
Identify the tolerated small differences
Know that Hanafi authorities consider very small deficiencies (e.g., half dirham per hundred, a danq) as negligible and do not give option. Note the ikhtilaf between Abu Yusuf and others.
Customary usage ('urf) plays a role in determining what is negligible.
5
Distinguish the treatment of increase (ziyada) and special cases
Increase is handled differently based on when it occurs (before/after measurement, before/after possession). Also note special rules for non-fungibles (qimi) and follow-up items (taba').
These are refinements of the general rule.

He gave an absolute ruling about the option in case of deficiency from what was specified. Qadikhan restricted it in his Fatawa saying: If someone bought a measured or weighed item on the condition that it is such-and-such, then found it less, the sale is valid for what is found. Does the buyer have an option? If the buyer did not take possession of the sold item, or took possession of part, he may return it. If he took possession of all, he has no option. End. Then know that in the case of deficiency, the share of the deficiency is dropped only if the sold item was not seen by him. If it was seen by him, then deception (ghurur) is absent. For this reason Qadikhan said in his Fatawa: If someone bought sawiq on the condition that the seller adds half a mann of ghee (samn) and they exchanged possession and the buyer is looking at it, then it becomes apparent that it has half a mann less? Actually the text says 'lahu binisaf mann' (it is half a mann less), the sale is valid and the buyer has no option because this is something known by sight. So when he sees it, ghurur is absent. It is like if someone bought soap on the condition that it is made from such-and-such a jar of oil, and it appeared that it is made from less than that, and the buyer is looking at the soap at the time of purchase. Similarly, if someone bought a shirt on the condition that it is made from ten cubits, and he is looking at it, and it appears to be from nine, the sale is valid and the buyer has no option, for what we said. End. He gave an absolute ruling in al-Ziyadat, and restricted it in al-Mujtaba to what does not fall under two measurements or weights, and what falls between them is not obliged to be returned. There is disagreement about the amount that falls between them: it is said half a dirham in a hundred, and it is said a danq in a hundred has no legal effect. From Abu Yusuf: a danq in ten is much. It is said what is less than a habba is excused in a dinar, and in the customary qafiz in our time, half a mann. End. And he restricted it with the condition that the increase was mixed in the sold item at the time of sale. For if it occurred in the sold item, as when wheat increases by moisture, then if it was pointed to (i.e., the specific heap) sold with the condition of measurement, the increase is for the seller if it occurred before measurement, and after it for the buyer, because the amount of the sold item is not apparent except by measurement. So the increase before measurement occurs on the seller's property, and after it on the buyer's property. If it was not pointed to, then what occurs after measurement but before possession is for the seller, and after possession for the buyer. The full detail of its branches is in al-Muhit. In al-Khaniyya: If someone sold land on the condition that it contains such-and-such palm trees, and the buyer found them deficient, the sale is valid and the buyer has an option: if he wishes he takes them for the price, and if he wishes he leaves, because trees enter in the sale of land as a follow-up (taba') and they have no separate share. Similarly, if someone sold a house on the condition that it contains such-and-such rooms, and found them deficient, the sale is valid and he has an option in this manner. Similarly, if someone sold a house on the condition that it contains such-and-such palm trees with their fruit, and sold the whole with their fruit, and there was a palm tree without fruit, the sale is invalid because the fruit has a share of the price. So when one tree is without fruit, the non-existent did not enter the sale, so the share of the remainder becomes unknown. Thus this is an inception of a contract on the remainder with an unknown price, so the sale is invalid. Just as if someone sold a slaughtered sheep and found its leg from the thigh cut off, the sale is invalid because the thigh has a specific portion? Actually the text says 'because the thigh has a quantity of qafizan determinately'? The text is unclear. End. And he restricted it with the condition that he specified (the quantity). From him: if someone sold a heap (sibra) on the condition that it is more than ten qafiz, and he finds it like that, the sale is valid. If he finds it exactly ten, he apportions the price? His statement: (Then know that in the case of deficiency...) He said in al-Nahr after quoting this and what was used as evidence from the words of al-Khaniyya: I say there is consideration in it, for the speech is about a sold item whose price parts are divided over its parts, and what is in al-Khaniyya...
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This lengthy passage from Qadikhan's Fatawa and other sources refines the general rule about the buyer's option (khiyar) when a measured or weighed item is found deficient. The initial absolute ruling is that if the item is less than specified, the buyer may return it only if he has not taken full possession. However, Qadikhan qualifies this: if the buyer actually saw the item at the time of purchase, then there is no deception (ghurur), and the buyer has no option. Examples: buying sawiq (parched barley) with added ghee, soap made from oil, or a shirt made from a certain length of cloth — if the buyer sees the item, he accepts it as is. This is because the defect is known by sight. The passage then discusses increase (ziyada): the general ruling is that small, negligible differences (like half a dirham in a hundred, or a danq) are overlooked and need not be returned. There is disagreement among early Hanafi authorities: some say half a dirham in a hundred is ignored, others say a danq (a small fraction). Abu Yusuf considered a danq in ten as significant. The increase is also categorized based on when it occurs — before or after measurement, and before or after possession. If the item is a specific heap (musharun ilayh), the increase before measurement belongs to the seller, after measurement to the buyer. For unspecified items, the rule shifts. The passage also covers non-fungibles (qimi): if the price per unit was not stated, a deficiency or excess invalidates the sale. But if the deficient item is a follow-up (taba') like trees in a land sale, the buyer still has an option. However, if the deficient item has its own share of the price (like fruit on trees), the sale is invalid due to uncertainty. The final part comments on a nuance from al-Nahr, noting that in some cases the price parts are divided over parts of the sold item. The text ends abruptly, indicating a continuing discussion.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyār): The right of the buyer (or seller) to rescind or confirm a sale due to a defect, deficiency, or other reason.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception; when a seller misleads the buyer about the quality or quantity of the item. Its presence gives the buyer an option.
قبض (qabḍ): Physical or constructive possession of the sold item by the buyer.
دائق (dā’iq): A small unit of weight or money, a fraction of a dirham or din? Here it appears as a small quantity (perhaps a danq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a buyer purchases a measured item, takes full possession, and later finds a deficiency that is considered small (e.g., half a dirham in a hundred)?
A) The sale is void because of the deficiency.
B) The buyer has an option to return because of the deficiency.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer has no option because full possession removes it and the deficiency is small.
D) The buyer must pay the full price but can sue for fraud.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the buyer's right to return deficient measured/weighed goods is conditional: full possession or sight of the item negates the option, and small customary differences are overlooked.

Buying a Measured Item Found Less: Effect of Visual Inspection
Ruling on khiyar for deficient measured goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a buyer finds the quantity of a measured or weighed item deficient, and identify when the option (khiyar) is available or lost.

Core Idea
If the buyer has seen the item, deficiency does not give an option because sight negates deception.

This lesson addresses a common sale scenario: you buy a item by weight or measure with a stated quantity, but later find it deficient. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that balances the buyer's right to what he contracted for with the reality of visual inspection. You will learn the default rule and the critical exception that turns on whether the buyer saw the item at purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of sale and condition
The text deals with a sale of a measured (mikyal) or weighed (mawzun) item where the buyer stipulates a specific quantity. Determine the object and the condition about quantity.
This is a condition in the contract, not a separate warranty.
2
Apply the default ruling on deficiency
When the found quantity is less than stated, the sale is valid for the actual quantity found. The price is reduced proportionally. No option exists if the buyer has taken full delivery.
The sale is sahih (valid) for the smaller quantity.
3
Check for delivery status
If the buyer has not taken delivery (qabd) of any portion, or has taken only part, he has the option (khiyar) to rescind the entire sale. Full delivery of the whole item removes the option.
Partial delivery still preserves the option.
4
Apply the exception for visual inspection
If the buyer actually saw the item at the time of purchase (mushahadah), then even if the quantity is deficient, he has no option. The sight negates any deception (ghurur), as the deficiency would have been apparent.
The examples: sawiq with ghee, soap with oil, shirt with cloth length.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اشترى مكيلاً أو موزوناً على أنه كذا فوجده أقل جاز البيع فيما وجد. وهل يخير المشتري؟ إن كان لم يقبض المشتري المبيع أو قبض البعض كان له أن يرده، وإن كان قبض الكل لا يخير اهـ. ثم اعلم أن في صورة النقصان إنما يسقط حصة النقصان إذا لم يكن المبيع مشاهداً له، فإن كان مشاهداً له انتفى الغرور ولهذا قال قاضيخان في فتاواه : اشترى سويقاً على أن البائع بمن من السمن وتقابضا والمشتري ينظر إليه فظهر أنه لته بنصف من، جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري لأن هذا مما يعرف بالعيان فإذا عاينه انتفى الغرور وهو كما لو اشترى صابوناً على أنه متخذ كذا جرة من الدهن فظهر أنه متخذ أقل . من ذلك والمشتري ينظر إلى الصابون وقت الشراء، وكذا لو اشترى قميصاً على أنه اتخذ من عشرة أذرع وهو ينظر إليه فإذا هو من تسعة جاز البيع ولا خيار للمشتري لما قلنا .اهـ.
If someone buys a measured or weighed item on the condition that it is a certain amount and finds it less, the sale is valid for what was found. Does the buyer have an option? If the buyer had not taken delivery of the sold item or had taken part, he may return it; if he had taken full delivery, he has no option. Then know that in the case of deficiency, the share of the deficiency is dropped only if the sold item was not seen by him. If it was seen by him, then deception is negated. For this reason Qadikhan said in his Fatawa: If someone bought sawiq (a kind of barley porridge) on the condition that the seller put a mann of ghee in it, they exchanged, and the buyer was looking at it, and it appeared that it was half a mann less, the sale is valid and the buyer has no option, because this is something known by sight, so when he saw it, deception is negated. It is like if someone bought soap on the condition that it was made with such-and-such a jar of oil, and it appeared that it was made with less, and the buyer was looking at the soap at the time of purchase. Similarly, if someone bought a shirt on the condition that it was made from ten cubits, and he was looking at it, and it turned out to be from nine, the sale is valid and the buyer has no option, for what we have said.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, commented upon by the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It addresses a common scenario in sales: a buyer purchases an item by weight or measure with a specified quantity, but finds it deficient. The default ruling is that the sale is valid for the actual quantity found, but the buyer has the option (khiyar) to rescind if he has not taken full delivery. However, there is a critical exception: if the buyer saw the item at the time of purchase (mushahadah), then no deception (gharar) exists, and the option is lost. The author supports this with examples from Qadikhan's Fatawa: sawiq (barley porridge) with ghee, soap made with oil, and a shirt made from cloth of a certain length. In all these, the buyer's visual inspection negates the possibility of deception, so the sale stands without option.

Key Terms
مبيع (mabī‘): The sold item, i.e., the object of sale.
خيار (khiyār): Option to rescind the sale.
غرور (ghurūr): Deception or fraud that nullifies the option when absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a buyer purchases a shirt on condition it is made from ten cubits, sees it, and finds it is only nine cubits after delivery, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is void.
B) The sale is valid and the buyer has the option to return it.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer has no option.
D) The buyer may keep it only if he pays less.

Key Takeaway
When a buyer has seen the item at the time of purchase, any subsequent deficiency in the specified quantity does not give him the option to rescind the sale, because sight negates deception (gharar).

Increase in Measured Goods: Ownership and De Minimis Tolerances
Ruling statement on increase in measured goods with de minimis rules and ownership conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings regarding increase in measured goods, distinguish between negligible and significant increases, and determine ownership of increase based on timing and type of sale.

Core Idea
In sales of measured goods, negligible increases are ignored; ownership of significant increases depends on when measurement occurs and whether the item is specified.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on increases in quantity when selling measured and weighed goods. The classical texts establish a de minimis rule: slight differences are overlooked. However, when the increase is more than negligible, its ownership is determined by precise rules tied to the timing of measurement and delivery. We will explore the thresholds, conditions, and the reasoning behind these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The ruling addresses increases in quantity of sold goods that are measured or weighed. The key question is: to whom does the extra amount belong, and when must it be returned?
2
State the de minimis rule
Slight increases (e.g., half a dirham per hundred) are considered negligible and are not returned. Different scholars give varying thresholds.
This prevents petty disputes over trivial amounts.
3
Understand the condition
The increase must be mixed in the sold item at the time of sale. If the increase occurs later (e.g., moisture absorption), ownership depends on the timing of measurement and delivery.
4
Distinguish between specific and generic sales
For a specific item (musharun ilayh) sold with condition of measurement, increase before measurement belongs to the seller; after measurement to the buyer. For non-specific items, the rule is based on delivery.
The basis: measurement reveals the true quantity, so pre-measurement increase is on seller's ownership.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a scenario (e.g., wheat absorbing moisture), determine whether the increase is negligible, whether it occurred before/after measurement, and whether the sale is of a specific item. Then decide ownership.
Practice with different combinations of conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في الزيادات وقيدها في المجتبى بما لا يدخل تحت الكيلين أو الوزنين وما يدخل بينهما لا يجب رده، واختلف في قدر ما يدخل بينهما فقيل نصف درهم في مائة، وقيل دائق في مائة لا حكم له، وعن أبي يوسف دانق في عشرة كثير وقيل ما دون حبة عفو في الدينار وفي القفيز المعتاد في زماننا نصف من اهـ. وقيد بكون الزيادة كانت مختلطة في المبيع وقت البيع لأنها لو حدثت في المبيع كما إذا زادت الحنطة بالبل فإن كان مشاراً إليه بيع بشرط الكيل تكون للبائع إن حدثت قبل الكيل، وإن بعده فللمشتري لأن قدر المبيع لا يظهر إلا بالكيل فتكون الزيادة قبل الكيل حادثة على ملك البائع، وبعده حادثة على ملك المشتري. وإن لم يكن مشاراً إليه فالحادثة بعد الكيل قبل القبض للبائع، وبعد القبض للمشتري وتمام تفريعاته في المحيط. وسيأتي أن القيمي إذا وجده ناقصاً أو زائداً فسد البيع إن لم يبين ثمن كل.
It was left unrestricted in al-Ziyadat and was restricted in al-Mujtaba to what does not fall under two measures or two weights, and what falls between them is not obligatory to return. There is disagreement on the amount that falls between them: it was said half a dirham in a hundred, it was said a da'iq (a small weight) in a hundred has no ruling, and from Abu Yusuf: a daniq (a small weight) in ten is much, and it was said less than a grain is pardoned in a dinar, and in the customary qafiz (a measure) in our time it is half a mann. It was restricted to the condition that the increase was mixed in the sold item at the time of sale, because if it occurred in the sold item, such as when wheat increases by moisture, then if it is pointed to (specific item) sold with the condition of measurement, it belongs to the seller if it occurred before measurement, and after measurement it belongs to the buyer, because the extent of the sold item is not known except by measurement, so the increase before measurement occurs in the ownership of the seller, and after it in the ownership of the buyer. If it is not pointed to, then what occurs after measurement but before delivery belongs to the seller, and after delivery to the buyer. The complete details of its branches are in al-Muhit. It will come later that if a non-fungible (qimi) item is found deficient or excessive, the sale becomes corrupt if the price of each is not specified.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling to cases of increase in measured or weighed goods. The author discusses the de minimis rule: slight differences (like half a dirham per hundred or a daniq per ten) are ignored and not returned. There is a key distinction: the increase must be mixed in the item at the time of sale. If the increase occurs later (e.g., wheat absorbing moisture), its ownership depends on when it happens relative to measurement and delivery. For specific items (musarun ilayh) sold by weight, increase before measurement belongs to seller, after to buyer. For unspecific items, the rule is based on delivery. The passage also references al-Mujtaba and al-Muhit for details.

Key Terms
زيادة (ziyādah): Increase in quantity of the sold item beyond what was stated.
مشار إليه (mushārun ilayh): A specific, identified item (e.g., a particular heap of wheat).
قيمي (qīmī): A non-fungible item (e.g., a unique good) that has no exact equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a buyer purchases a specific heap of wheat with the condition of measurement, and the wheat increases by moisture before measurement, to whom does the increase belong?
A) The buyer
B) The seller
C) It is split equally
D) The sale becomes void

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, negligible increases in measured goods are tolerated, but significant increases are allocated based on whether the sale is of a specific item and the timing of measurement and delivery.

Sale of Land with Trees and Heap Sales: Khiyar and Invalidity
Covers khiyar for deficiency in land with trees/houses, invalidity when part is missing, and ruling on heap sales with quantity condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on khiyar for deficient trees/rooms in land/house sales, distinguish between when deficiency gives an option versus invalidates the sale, and apply the Hanafi position on heap sales with quantity conditions, including the dispute with Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
If the deficient item has no apportioned share, the buyer has an option; if it has a share, the sale corrupts.

This passage from al-Khaniyah addresses two important sale scenarios. First, when land or a house is sold with a stated number of trees or rooms and some are missing, we need to determine whether the deficiency gives the buyer an option or invalidates the contract entirely. Second, when a heap of grain is sold with a condition on its quantity, the ruling depends on whether the condition is met. An intra-Hanafi dispute on the latter highlights the importance of price certainty in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of items
Distinguish between items that are accessories (tab‘) without an independent share of the price (qist) and items that have a separate qist. Trees without fruit and rooms are accessories; fruit-bearing trees have a qist.
2
Apply the ruling to deficiency
If the deficient item has no qist, the sale is valid with khiyar (option) for the buyer. If it has a qist and is missing, the sale becomes fasid (corrupt) because the price becomes unknown.
The sheep leg analogy illustrates this: the leg has a specific portion of the price.
3
Analyze the heap sale condition
For a heap (sabra) sold with a condition like 'more than ten qafiz' or 'less than ten', if the condition is exactly met or violated (e.g., found exactly ten or more when stated 'more than ten'), the sale is invalid according to the majority Hanafi view.
The condition affects the price apportionment.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that the heap sale is valid even if the quantity condition fails, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad rule invalidity. Understand the basis: Abu Yusuf sees the condition as non-essential.
The mufta bihi follows the majority.
5
Apply the principle to the house length condition
When a house is sold with a condition on its length (e.g., ten cubits), the sale is always valid because length is a description that does not have a separate qist.
This confirms the general rule: non-divisible descriptions do not affect validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : باع أرضاً على أن فيها كذا كذا نخلة فوجدها المشتري ناقصة جاز البيع ويخير المشتري إن شاء أخذها الثمن وإن شاء ترك لأن الشجر يدخل في بيع الأرض تبعاً ولا يكون له قسط وكذا لو باع داراً على أن فيها كذا كذا بيتاً فوجدها ناقصة جاز البيع ويخير على هذا الوجه، وكذا لو باع داراً على أن فيها كذا كذا نخلة عليها ثمارها فباع الكل بثمارها وكان فيها نخلة غير مثمرة فسد البيع لأن الثمر له قسط من الثمن، فإذا كانت الواحدة غير مثمرة لم يدخل المعدوم في البيع فصارت حصة الباقي مجهولة فيكون هذا ابتداء عقد في الباقي بثمن مجهول فيفسد البيع كما لو باع شاة مذبوحة فإذا رجلها من الفخذ مقطوعة فسد البيع لأن الفخذ له جملة القفزان على التعيين لأنه لو سماها على الإبهام كما الثمن اهـ. وقيد بكونه سمى . من لو باع صبرة على أنها أكثر من عشرة أقفزة، فإن وجدها كذلك جاز البيع، وإن وجدها عشرة أو أقل من عشرة لا يجوز البيع ولو باعها على أنها أقل من عشرة فوجدها كذلك جاز، وإن وجدها عشرة أو أكثر لا يجوز البيع، وعن أبي يوسف أنه يجوز البيع. ولو اشترى داراً على أنها عشرة أذرع جاز البيع في الوجوه كلها كذا في الخانية.
And in al-Khaniyah: If he sold land on the condition that it contains such-and-such a number of palm trees, and the buyer finds it deficient, the sale is valid and the buyer has the option: if he wishes he takes it for the price, and if he wishes he leaves it, because the trees enter the sale of land as a follow (tab') and they have no apportioned share. Similarly, if he sold a house on the condition that it contains such-and-such a number of rooms, and found it deficient, the sale is valid and he has the option in this manner. Similarly, if he sold a house on the condition that it contains such-and-such a number of palm trees with their fruit, and sold the whole with its fruit, and there was a palm tree that was not fruit-bearing, the sale becomes corrupt (fasid), because the fruit has an apportioned share of the price. So when one tree is not fruit-bearing, the non-existent does not enter the sale, so the share of the rest becomes unknown, and this becomes a new contract for the remainder with an unknown price, so the sale becomes corrupt, just as if he sold a slaughtered sheep and found its leg cut from the thigh, the sale becomes corrupt, because the thigh has a specific portion of the price. And it is restricted to his specifying. From him: If he sold a heap (sabra) on the condition that it is more than ten qafiz, and he finds it so, the sale is valid; if he finds it ten or less, the sale is not valid. If he sold it on the condition that it is less than ten, and finds it so, it is valid; if he finds it ten or more, it is not valid. And from Abu Yusuf: the sale is valid. And if he bought a house on the condition that it is ten cubits, the sale is valid in all these ways, as in al-Khaniyah.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniyah addresses two related scenarios. First, when land is sold with a stated number of trees or a house with a stated number of rooms, and the counted items are deficient: the sale is valid and the buyer has the option to take or cancel, because trees and rooms are considered follow (tab') to the land/house and have no independent share of the price. However, if the trees have fruit that gives them a share, then a missing fruit-bearing tree makes the sale corrupt due to unknown price distribution, analogous to selling a sheep with a missing leg. Second, the passage discusses sale of a heap (sabra) of grain with a condition on its quantity: if stated as more than ten qafiz and found exactly ten or less, the sale is invalid according to the Hanafi view, but Abu Yusuf permits it. Similarly if stated as less than ten and found ten or more, invalid. The final sentence clarifies that a house sold with a condition on length (ten cubits) is valid in all cases, as length is a description, not principle.

Key Terms
تبع (tab‘): A follow or accessory that is included in the sale without independent consideration.
صبرة (ṣabra): A heap of grain or similar commodity sold as a whole.
قسط (qisṭ): An apportioned share of the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a house is sold with the condition that it contains a certain number of palm trees with their fruit, and one tree is found non-fruit-bearing, what is the ruling?
A) The sale is valid and the buyer has an option.
B) The sale is corrupt (fasid).
C) The sale is void (batil).
D) The buyer must take it at a reduced price.

Key Takeaway
In sales with stated conditions, if a deficient item has no apportioned share, the buyer has an option; if it has a share, the sale becomes corrupt.
